URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS52351842	2019-03-18 22:17:58	3504	species	Common Poorwill	Phalaenoptilus nuttallii			3				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Clark	US-NV-003	US-NV_943	33			Buck Spring Canyon, Spring Mtns.	L514246	P	36.3399842	-115.7789040	2008-04-06	21:00:00	obsr89980	S3773211	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	360	7.242			1		0	1	0		ZTHA: --[Describe in as much detail as possible the bird which you saw without referring to field guides.  Emphasize particularly the marks used to identify the bird, but include a complete description of the bird.  Include size, overall shape, plumage and color pattern; details on shape or size of bill, wings, and tail.  Describe what you are actually observing in the field.]--The bird was a fairly large raptor with fairly rectangular, parallel edged wings and a long tail.  Overall the plumage was black on the body, underwing coverts, and tail.  The flight feathers were silvery gray, underneath.  At first glance the bird was quickly dimissed as a Turkey Vulture to which it closely resembled in size, shape, and plumage.  When I put my binoculars on it a single bold solid white subterminal band was clearly visible on the underside of the otherwise black tail.  ------[Describe the bird's overall behavior:]--The bird came gliding across the sky from the direction of the crest of the Spring Mts./Bonanza Peak area.  If glided quickly and directly on a very slight dihedral.  Within 15 seconds or so it disappeared behind a ridge to the northwest.----[Describe the habitat in which you observed the bird:]--Area is dominated by farily dry Pinon-Juniper woodland with contiguous stands of mature Ponderosa and a few White Firs following the more mesic drainage bottoms.  Because the bird was gliding high over a broad area I can comment of specific micro-habitat.  The sighting occurred very near a fairly wet spring with a particularly mesic ponderosa canyon directly above.  The area below the crest, from which the bird may have came, is dominated by Ponderosa, White Fir, and possibly a few Bristlecones (as IDed at great length through binoculars).--------[Describe the bird's calls or sounds, if heard, including method of delivery, i.e., from perch, in flight, etc.:]  None given--------[List similar species and how you eliminated them:]--As stated, my initial impression was of a Turkey Vulture.  Upon closer inspection this was easily eliminated based on the solid white subterminal band on the underside of the tail.  The fast direct flight and shallow dihedral were also somewhat characteristic of a TV.  Common Black-hawk was easily eliminated based on the long tail, relatively narrow and straight wings, black tail tip, and distinctly two-toned underwings.  The flight feathers were too dark and the tail pattern all wrong for a dark rough-legged.  The bold single white tail band was inconsistent with any type of Red-tailed hawk including Harlan's.  The flight feathers were also too dark gray/silver and the wing shape wrong (too straight)------[Prior experience with this and similar species:]--I have extensive experience with Zone-tailed Hawk from New Mexico, Arizona, and once Texas.  I've lived for four years in southern NM where I focused much birding effort on the southwestern mountains where the species borders on common.  I have seen it many times in the Chiricahuas and other ranges of SE Arizona as well.  I am also very experienced with Turkey Vulture.----[Optical equipment:]--Swift Eaglet 10X42 roof prism binoculars----

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS22371196	2018-11-28 14:50:13	3504	species	Common Poorwill	Phalaenoptilus nuttallii			2				United States	US	Nevada	US-NV	Washoe	US-NV-031	US-NV_2211	15			Lower Marlette Creek area (Washoe Co.; Marlette Lake Trail below the switchbacks)	L866938	H	39.1678344	-119.9265701	2004-07-10	04:00:00	obsr16674	S1693546	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	210	4.828		2	1	G189367	0	1	0		John Anderson called me Friday night to discuss plans for a July big day that we had talked about earlier.  We iterated through the state checklist and discussed where the best place to find each bird would be.  I wrote down some species that I had no idea where to find (where does one see Violet-green Swallows in July near Reno?) and planned to do some research and scouting.  "So, when do you want to go?" I asked.  "Is tomorrow OK?", John replied.  So much for planning, but I immediately agreed to have him pick me up at 3:00 AM to head to Lake Tahoe.  On to the birds:----Our general strategy was to pick up mountain birds in the morning, shorebirds and waterbirds in the afternoon, and riparian sage birds in the evening.  We had a difficult time leaving the spectacle of shorebirds at Carson Lake, so our riparian birds were duly neglected. Big misses include Bullock's Oriole, Tree Swallow, House Wren, Townsend's Solitaire, Bushtit,Brewer's Sparrow and Red-breasted Sapsucker.  Not-so-big misses include Blue Grouse, Canyon Wren, Rock Wren, Pinyon Jay, Wood Duck, Black-throated Sparrow, Violet-green Swallow, and Sage Sparrow.  Unexpected surprises included a calling SAW-WHET OWL at Chimney Beach, 20 high-flying WHITE PELICANS over Mt. Rose Meadows, 2 HOODED MERGANSERS at Virginia Lake, and a pair of breeding-plumage COMMON LOONS(!!) on Big Soda Lake in Fallon.
